State v. Murray


No. A-3856-87T4


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


240 N.J. Super. 378; 573 A.2d 488; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 144


February 14, 1990, Submitted  
April 27, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]  Approved for Publication May 7, 1990. As Corrected.
Certification denied by State v. Murray, 122 N.J. 334, 585 A.2d 350, 1990 N.J. LEXIS 1032 (1990)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant inmate appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Middlesex County that convicted him of attempted escape, possession of implements of escape, and criminal restraint of a correction officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant inmate was convicted of aggravated sexual assault, so the trial court committed him to a treatment center. While at the center, defendant took a guard hostage by holding a shank to him and claimed that he wanted to escape. Defendant was subsequently convicted of attempted escape, possession of implements of escape, and criminal restraint of a correction officer. Defendant alleged that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that defendant had the burden of proving by a preponderance of evidence that he suffered from a mental disease under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:4-2. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction. The jury instruction was incorrect because once defendant came forward with evidence of a mental disease, the burden of disproving such disease fell upon the state. The error was harmless though because there was no competent evidence that defendant suffered from a mental disease, which would have negated the knowing element of the attempted escape offense. In addition, the evidence against defendant was overwhelming.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant inmate's convictions for attempted escape, possession of implements of escape, and criminal restraint of a correction officer. The trial court's erroneous jury instruction regarding the burden of proof of a mental disease was harmless because there was no evidence that defendant suffered from a mental disease.

Holloway v. State


No. A-4219-88T5F


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


239 N.J. Super. 554; 571 A.2d 1324; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 95


November 28, 1989, Argued  
March 16, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication April 3, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Hunterdon County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of decisions of the Superior Court, Hunterdon County (New Jersey), which granted partial summary judgment to third-party defendant manufacturers and issued rulings limiting the state's right to contribution, in an action by plaintiff inmate seeking damages for personal injuries suffered when she dove into a swimming pool at a correctional facility.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff inmate was injured when she dove into a prison swimming pool. She brought an action against the state seeking damages for her injuries. The state filed third-party claims against third-party defendants, manufacturers, and later amended its claims to add a direct claim for medical costs. The trial court granted partial summary judgment to third-party defendants on the state's claims, and issued rulings limiting the state's right to contribution on comparative negligence grounds. The state sought review of its decision. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court stated that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:9-2b, which barred recovery against a public entity for strict products liability, afforded no basis for barring such recovery in favor of a public entity, and that the state's claim for contribution need not assert the same theory of liability as plaintiff's claim. The court stated that the statute of limitations did not run against the state. The court affirmed the trial court's rulings that determination of the state's contribution and medical expense claims had to include consideration of the negligence of the state and plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's summary judgment for third-party defendant manufacturers on the state's claims in plaintiff inmate's personal injury action because the state could claim strict products liability and its claims were not time-barred and affirmed the trial court's rulings that the negligence of the state and plaintiff had to be considered in determining the state's right to recover from third-party defendants.

Johnson v. Mountainside Hosp.


No. A-2293-87T1


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


239 N.J. Super. 312; 571 A.2d 318; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 68


December 12, 1989, Argued  
March 12, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]  Approved for Publication March 19, 1990. As Corrected; Second Correction.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executor-survivor sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Essex County, which limited respondent hospital's liability in appellant's wrongful death and survival action by the charitable immunity statute under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:53A-8.


OVERVIEW: A jury found respondent hospital 20 percent negligent in appellant executor-survivor's wrongful death and survival action that arose from the death of his wife. Because respondent's liability was limited by the charitable immunity statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:53A-8, the judgment against respondent was limited to $ 10,000. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed. The court rejected appellant's contention that the statute, as applied to hospitals, was special legislation and violated the due process and equal protection clauses of the United States Constitution. The constitutionality of the statute was well settled. The court also rejected appellant's contention that the statute was inapplicable because his wife's care was paid for by medical insurance. The fact of insurance did not change the impact of the statute or confer additional rights upon an injured beneficiary; every user of a non-profit hospital was a beneficiary of its works. The court held that the trial court properly ruled that respondent could not be treated as an equipment lessor to whom strict liability applied. Imposition of strict liability on respondent would have been against public policy.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment in which respondent hospital's liability to appellant executor-survivor was limited by New Jersey's charitable immunity statute was affirmed because the statute was constitutional, and the fact of insurance did not confer additional rights on a beneficiary of respondent's works.

 

1076 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Integrity Ins. Co.


No. A-5647-88T1F, A-5649-88T1F


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


240 N.J. Super. 480; 573 A.2d 928; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 140


January 18, 1990, Argued  
March 6, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, owners and officers, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Bergen County (New Jersey), which denied defendants' motion to dismiss for lack of standing. Pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:30C-15, the trial court allowed plaintiff liquidator to assert claims on behalf of the insolvent insurance company and claims on behalf of the company's creditors, policyholders, and other beneficiaries.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff liquidator brought causes of action against defendants, owners and officers, on behalf of the insolvent insurance company's creditors, policyholders, and other beneficiaries pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:30C-15. The trial court denied defendants' motion to dismiss certain counts for lack of standing. The court affirmed the decision regarding standing, but remanded the case for institution of a class action proceeding. The court held that plaintiff could prosecute the claims on behalf of the company's creditors and policyholders provided that the objective of such action was to increase the assets of the estate of the insolvent insurance company to which the creditors and policyholders could look for satisfaction of their debt. The court held that plaintiff could not assert personal claims of the creditors and policyholders. The court found that the creditors and policyholders could bring separate causes of action for their personal claims against defendants. The court ruled that the best way to afford due process protections to all parties was to provide the claimants with an opportunity to participate in a class action type proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the portion of the superior court's decision that allowed plaintiff liquidator to bring the action against defendants, owners and officers, on behalf of the insolvent insurance company's policyholders, creditors, and other beneficiaries. The court remanded the case for the trial court to institute a class action to prevent diseconomies and multiplicity and to ensure the basic fairness requirement.

State v. Todd


No. A-2742-88T5


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


238 N.J. Super. 445; 570 A.2d 20; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 39


November 29, 1989, Submitted  
February 6, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication February 21, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Somerset County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, imposing concurrent prison sentences for drug related offenses, after appellant entered in to plea agreement with appellee state, and pleaded guilty. Appellant raised constitutional challenges to certain parts of the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1986, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1 et seq., and claimed it impermissibly deprived the trial court from imposing a lesser sentence.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a plea agreement, appellant pleaded guilty to four counts of distribution of drugs, two counts of possession of drugs with intent to distribute, and two counts of possession of drugs. Appellee state recommended a prison sentence with a period of parole ineligibility. The trial judge merged the offenses of possession and possession with intent to distribute into the distribution charges and imposed concurrent prison sentences with a parole disqualifier, pursuant to the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1986, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1 et seq. Appellant sought review, and the court reversed the merger order, remanded for resentencing on those offenses, and otherwise affirmed the convictions and prison sentences. The court held that the trial judge erred by merging the convictions, and by imposing a mandatory laboratory fee on only two convictions, but rejected appellant's constitutional challenges. The court held that § 2C:35-1 et seq. was constitutional, because although it granted some sentencing discretion to the prosecutor during plea bargaining, it did not remove the judiciary's final authority concerning the fairness of the plea bargain.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order merging drug related offenses of possession, and possession with intent to distribute, into distribution charges; remanded for resentencing on those offenses; and otherwise affirmed drug convictions and prison sentences. The court held that the trial judge erred by merged convictions, and by imposing a mandatory laboratory fee on only two convictions, but rejected appellant's constitutional challenges.

Urban v. Planning Bd. of Manasquan, Monmouth County


Nos. A-1062-88T2, A-1113-88T2, A-1123-88T2, A-1311-88T2, A-1315-88T2


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


238 N.J. Super. 105; 569 A.2d 275; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 30


November 21, 1989, Submitted  
January 23, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication February 13, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board appealed the judgments from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Monmouth County, which set aside defendant's denial of plaintiff applicants' subdivision applications to create two non-conforming lots from one lot, and determined the fees for defendant's secretary and an engineer.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff applicants filed subdivision applications with defendant board to create two non-conforming lots from one lot. A lower court entered five judgments setting aside defendant's denial of plaintiffs' applications. Defendant appealed. The court reversed the lower court's judgment, holding that two case precedents did not compel this result. The court did not agree with the lower court that the record did not support an analysis under another New Jersey appellate decision because the structures as described by the judge in the appellate decision relied on by the court resembled those in plaintiffs' applications far more than those in the two case precedents, which the court rejected. The court was satisfied that defendant properly distinguished the two disfavored case precedents from the applications before it and properly ruled that plaintiffs were not entitled to a subdivision without a variance. The court found, therefore, that the lower court erred in its reversal of defendant's decision. The court was persuaded that the lower court's ruling on defendant's fee applications was well supported, and the court therefore affirmed that portion of the lower court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a lower court's judgments setting aside defendant board's denial of plaintiff applicants' subdivision applications because defendant properly distinguished two case precedents, which were misinterpreted, and properly ruled that plaintiffs were not entitled to a subdivision without a variance. The court affirmed the lower court's disposition of defendant's fee applications because the lower court's ruling was well supported.

State v. Anaya


No. A-4240-87T4


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


238 N.J. Super. 31; 568 A.2d 1208; 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 24


November 20, 1989, Submitted  
January 8, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication February 7, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court, Law Division, Passaic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her convictions for possession of a controlled dangerous substance, heroin, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:35-10a(1), 2C:2-6, possession with intent to distribute, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:35-5a(1), b(3), 2C:2-6, and possession with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of school property, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:35-5a, 2C:35-7, 2C:2-6 and the opinion from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a controlled dangerous substance, heroin, possession with intent to distribute, and possession with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of school property. The court also rejected appellant's other points of error, including defendant's sentencing contention.

1081 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mazurek


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


237 N.J. Super. 231; 567 A.2d 277; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 424


September 11, 1989, Argued  
December 8, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his guilty plea for driving while intoxicated, defendant appealed an interlocutory order of the Matawan Municipal Court (New Jersey), denying his motion to suppress evidence obtained at a roadblock. Defendant claimed that the sobriety checkpoint violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV and N.J. Const. art. I, § 7, because it was not justified by empirical data showing the public's safety warranted the checkpoint.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested at a sobriety checkpoint on the highway. He pleaded guilty to driving while intoxicated after the trial court denied his motion to suppress evidence based on the illegality of the roadblock. On appeal, defendant challenged that the checkpoint violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV and N.J. Const. art. I, § 7 because it was not justified by empirical data showing that the public's safety warranted the checkpoint. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the checkpoint was a properly targeted location that was efficacious, and that a rational basis existed for the site selection. Therefore, the warrantless stopping of defendant's vehicle that led to the discovery of his intoxicated condition was reasonable under U.S. Const. amend. IV and N.J. Const. art. I, § 7. Because the state sustained the burden of proving the validity of the seizure, the trial court's order denying the motion to suppress was affirmed, and the judgment of conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for driving while intoxicated. The court held that defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained when he was arrested at a sobriety checkpoint was properly denied because a rational basis existed for the roadblock. Therefore it did not violate defendant's Fourth Amendment rights or his rights under the New Jersey Constitution.

Petition of Soto


Nos. A-6203-86T7, A-1641-87T7


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


236 N.J. Super. 303; 565 A.2d 1088; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 381


October 31, 1988, Argued  
October 23, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from Superior Court, Chancery Division, Atlantic County and the State Casino Control Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff casino attorney appealed the ruling of defendants, a state and its Casino Control Commission, Department of Law and Public Safety and Division of Gaming Enforcement, that under § 138 of the Casino Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-138, she could not provide free legal services to a political party committee, and the judgment of the Superior Court, Atlantic County (New Jersey) declaring that § 138 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a state and its Casino Control Commission, Department of Law and Public Safety and Division of Gaming Enforcement, ruled that § 138 of the Casino Control Act (the CCA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-138, prohibited plaintiff casino attorney from providing free legal services to a political party. She sought a judgment declaring the CCA unconstitutional in that it interfered with her rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, was vague and overbroad, and discriminated against casino key employees in violation of the Equal Protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the New Jersey Constitution. The trial court granted defendants' motion to dismiss or for summary judgment. The court on appeal affirmed, holding that: the state had a compelling interest in preventing corruption by the casino industry and a broad prohibition on all political contributions from casino key employees was necessary to achieve that interest; the CCA was narrowly tailored only to casino key employees; the terms "thing of value" and "professional services" were well known and defined such that it was clear what conduct was prohibited; and legal services were a "thing of value."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling that the Casino Control Act (CCA) prohibited plaintiff casino attorney from providing free legal services to a political party, and the order dismissing her action for a judgment declaring the CCA unconstitutional. The court held that the CCA was narrowly tailored to serve the state's compelling interest in preventing corruption of the political process by the casino industry.

Division of Motor Vehicles v. Granziel

Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division

October 11, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Final Decision of the Division of Motor Vehicles.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought relief from a decision by the director of the Division of Motor Vehicles (New Jersey), which upheld a prior determination suspending appellant's driver's license for a one-year period because appellant suffered from epileptic seizures.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a determination of the Division of Motor Vehicles (DMV) that suspended the driver's license of appellant driver for a one-year period due to appellant's epilepsy. The court held that the nature of appellant's seizure's required the suspension of his driving privileges under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:3-10. The time frame between onset of a seizure and unconsciousness was too short and appellant's seizures were not controlled. The court was concerned over the wording and application of N.J. Admin. Appropriate consideration had been given as to whether appellant could drive an automobile without significant risk to himself or others. The court found valid the presumption that a person who had not been seizure free for one-year prior to a license suspension was not sufficiently controlled to permit that person to drive. The one-year suspension period was not excessive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of the Division of Motor Vehicles to suspend appellant driver's privilege to drive for a one-year period. The evidence supported a finding that appellant could not drive an automobile without significant risk to himself or others.

State v. Whalen


No. A-2716-87T8


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


235 N.J. Super. 506; 563 A.2d 457; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 332


March 13, 1989, Submitted  
August 16, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant police officer was convicted of perjury, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:28-1, by the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Mercer County. Defendant appealed, contending that materiality, as an element of the offense of perjury, should have been submitted to the jury for final determination rather than being allocated to the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant police officer told state police that his girlfriend could provide information about a loansharking operation in her luncheonette. Defendant and his girlfriend met with state police on numerous occasions, and arranged an eavesdropping device in the luncheonette. When the girlfriend was called to testify before the state grand jury, she denied meeting with state police and denied knowledge of the loansharking operation. Defendant also testified that he and his girlfriend had not met with state police. Defendant was subsequently convicted of perjury under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:28-1, which required the testimony to be material, and which stated that materiality was a question of law. Defendant appealed, contending that materiality, as an element of the offense of perjury, should have been submitted to the jury for final determination rather than being allocated to the trial court. The court affirmed the conviction. After examining historical authorities, the court concluded that the legislature was free to assign the question of materiality to the trial court, and that the trial court had correctly concluded that defendant's false testimony was material.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant police officer's conviction, holding, after an examination of authorities, that the issue of the materiality of the testimony alleged to be perjured was correctly allocated as an issue of law for the court by the legislature, and that the issue was correctly decided by the trial court in this case. The court did not address defendant's other contentions.

State in Interest of W.M.


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


237 N.J. Super. 111; 567 A.2d 217; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 432


May 15, 1989, Submitted  
August 16, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A juvenile appealed the adjudication of delinquency for conduct which would have constituted conspiracy to distribute cocaine if committed by an adult and the imposition of mandatory penalties under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1.


OVERVIEW: Juvenile was adjudicated delinquent for the offense of conspiracy to distribute cocaine. The court assessed a penalty of $ 1,000, six-month postponement of driving privileges, and a $ 25 forensic laboratory fee which the juvenile challenged. The court reversed the penalties, holding that they did not apply to conspiracy to commit a drug violation. The court held that the legislature did not intend to include the crime of conspiracy under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1. The fact that the object of the conspiracy was a crime under the Act, or that conspiracy was graded in accordance with crimes for which the penalties could be imposed was not sufficient to support the theory that conspiracy alone constituted an offense under the act for which mandatory penalties applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the imposing of penalties because conspiracy was not an offense defined under statute for which mandatory penalties could be imposed. The plain language of the statutes in question indicated penalties did not apply to convictions of conspiracy since conspiracy was not an offense defined under applicable chapters of the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act.

Appellant contended that appellee insurance association was a successor to the Automobile Insurance Plan, and that he had a statutory right to full annual commissions upon cancellation of the policies. In affirming the lower court's dismissal of his complaint, the court held that appellee was entitled to have the complaint dismissed as a matter of law because the legislature, in enacting subdivisions j and k to § 17:22-6.14a, intended that commissions for unearned premiums were not to be paid for policies that were cancelled for nonpayment. The court stated that § 17:22-6.14a and its amendments did not result in harsh or oppressive consequences, did not tread on vested rights, and did not violate the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that dismissed appellant insurance producer's complaint. The court held that appellee was entitled to have the complaint dismissed as a matter of law because the legislature, in amending the statute that governed the payment of commissions, intended that commissions for unearned premiums were not to be paid for policies that were cancelled for nonpayment.

State v. Wright


No. A-4310-87T1


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


235 N.J. Super. 97; 561 A.2d 659; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 293


April 10, 1989, Submitted  
July 20, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Camden County, New Jersey, which convicted her of maintaining a premises as a house of prostitution under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-12c.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for promoting prostitution under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-1 and for maintaining a premises as a house of prostitution under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-12c. Defendant pleaded guilty and was convicted of maintaining a premises as a house of prostitution. Defendant claimed that her conviction was invalid because the statutes were unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court found that the statutes were not vague because a female employee offered to remove her clothing, sit on the lap of a male customer, and masturbate him to orgasm in exchange for a fee. This conduct constituted sexual activity. The court also held that the applicable statutes were not constitutionally overbroad. The statutes enforced against defendant did not impinge upon any U.S. Const. amend. I rights. Sexual activity for money was not protected by a constitutional right to privacy or a state-granted right to privacy. The court found no danger that the statutes would be used to penalize private consensual sexual activity. Moreover, there was no constitutional impediment to the prosecution of the activity for which defendant pleaded guilty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of maintaining a premises as a house of prostitution. The court held that there was no constitutional impediment to the prosecution of the activity for which defendant pleaded guilty.

State v. Grimes


No. A-3407-87T7


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


235 N.J. Super. 75; 561 A.2d 647; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 303


February 8, 1989, Argued  
July 19, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Burlington County (New Jersey), which convicted him of official misconduct in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:30-2.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of official misconduct in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:30-2 after he allegedly accepted a fee for removing tenants as constable, knowing that he was not permitted to do so. On appeal, the court reversed and dismissed the indictment. Expert opinion testimony on matters of domestic law should not have been admitted, and the expert witness, as the chief judge of the vicinage, should not have been allowed to testify. Given the weight of his judicial office, his testimony as to the nature of the office of constable, its scope, and responsibilities was tremendously harmful. The law of defendant's duties in tenant removals was so uncertain that it was presented to the jury as a matter of disputed fact. That degree of uncertainty showed that the law did not give a person of ordinary intelligence fair warning of what conduct was proscribed, thus, it was fundamentally unfair to subject defendant to a criminal prosecution.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was reversed, and the indictment was dismissed. Expert opinion testimony on domestic law by the vicinage's chief judge was prejudicial. The law relating to the office of constable was so unclear that it was unfair to subject defendant to prosecution for official misconduct because there was no fair warning of what constituted proscribed conduct.

 

1091 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Drew Associates of N.J., L.P. v. Travisano


No. A-1498-88T1


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


235 N.J. Super. 194; 561 A.2d 1177; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 296


April 24, 1989, Argued  
July 17, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from Superior Court, Chancery Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant limited partnership sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Middlesex County (New Jersey), which granted summary judgment to respondent New Jersey Department of Community Affairs in appellant's suit challenging the constitutionality of the Cooperative Recording Act of New Jersey, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:8D-1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant limited partnership owned an apartment complex that was to be converted into a cooperative and filed suit challenging the constitutionality of the Cooperative Recording Act of New Jersey (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:8D-1 et seq. The trial court held that the Act was constitutional and granted summary judgment to respondent New Jersey Department of Community Affairs. On appeal, the court reversed as to a revenue-raising transfer tax imposed solely on post-Act cooperatives because it violated equal protection. Only the costs associated with maintaining public recording facilities could be properly assessed against post-Act cooperatives by taxation; the case was remanded for determination of that amount. The court affirmed the rest of the judgment because the Act was reasonably related to a legitimate legislative purpose and was not arbitrary or discriminatory. Even if N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:8D-2 was deemed to impose duplicate taxation, the legislature was constitutionally empowered to enact such a statute. Because the cooperative board could not unreasonably withhold its consent to transfers, the Act was not contrary to the policy that real estate be freely alienable.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment for respondent New Jersey Department of Community Affairs was affirmed because the Cooperative Recording Act of New Jersey was rationally related to a legitimate legislative purpose, with the exception of the imposition of a revenue-raising tax on post-act cooperatives. That portion of the judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for determination of the proper tax assessment attributable to administrative costs.

 

1092 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bulu


No. A-2605-88T5F


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


234 N.J. Super. 331; 560 A.2d 1250; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 269


May 9, 1989, Argued  
July 11, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication July 21, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Warren County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Warren County (New Jersey), which held that the mandatory Drug Enforcement and Demand Reduction penalty could be assessed, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-15a, against defendant where he had been charged with drug offenses and subsequently admitted into a pretrial intervention program.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was charged with various drug violations, he was accepted into the pretrial intervention (PTI) program. Defendant was then required to pay a mandatory Drug Enforcement and Demand Reduction (DEDR) penalty as a condition to admission to the PTI program. Subsequently, defendant sought a judicial declaration that the portion of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-15 mandating DEDR penalties was unconstitutional. The court held that the DEDR penalties were not punitive in nature where they served a general rehabilitative and preventive function and, therefore, the penalties could be imposed absent a finding of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court further found that the imposition of DEDR penalties did not violate substantive due process because the law requiring the penalties was not unreasonable or arbitrary where it sought to serve a general rehabilitative or preventive function. Finally, the court held that imposition of DEDR penalties did not violate equal protection where N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-15d and e provided an avenue whereby a person unable to pay a DEDR penalty may gain admission into, or avoid termination from, PTI. As such, the court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order, holding that where the statute provided an avenue whereby a person unable to pay a DEDR penalty could gain admission into, or avoid termination from, the pretrial intervention program, the penalties did not violate equal protection.

 

1093 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Moiseyev v. New Jersey Racing Com'n


No. A-1338-88T3


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


239 N.J. Super. 1; 570 A.2d 988; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 496


June 13, 1989, Argued  
June 28, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
  Approved for Publication March 8, 1990.
Certification denied by Moiseyev v. New Jersey Racing Com., 117 N.J. 666, 569 A.2d 1358, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 1438 (1989)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the decision of the New Jersey Racing Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant harness driver sought review of the decision of an administrative law judge that affirmed the decision of respondent New Jersey Racing Commission that found appellant had violated N.J. Admin. Code tit. 13, § 71-20.10(b) by driving unsatisfactorily due to lack of effort, contending that the regulation was unconstitutionally vague, that the decision was against the weight of the evidence and that his 30 day suspension was unwarranted.


OVERVIEW: Respondent New Jersey Racing Commission, pursuant to a violation of N.J. Admin Code tit. 13, § 71-20.10(b), suspended appellant harness driver for 30 days for unsatisfactory driving due to lack of effort. Appellant sought review and an administrative law judge (ALJ) affirmed appellant's suspension. Appellant challenged the decision, contending that the regulation was unconstitutionally vague, that his due process rights were violated during initial hearings, that he was not given adequate notice of exceptions to the ALJ's recommendations, that the ALJ had improperly denied appellant 's presentation of his prior testimony at trial, and that his 30 day suspension was not warranted. The court affirmed and held that the language of the regulation was not so vague that a person of ordinary intelligence was unable to discern what was required, that appellant's due process allegations were answered by his trial de novo before the ALJ, that appellant should have been served with timely notice, but that the error was harmless because he had already filed written exceptions to respondent's findings, and that the regulations had only permitted a suspension of 10 days.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision and deferred to the discretion of the administrative law judge who found in favor of respondent New Jersey Racing Commission because when viewed in the context of the racing industry, the subjective evaluation of appellant harness driver's performance was necessary to promote public confidence. Appellant had a fair hearing; however, the court found the regulation had permitted only a 10 day suspension.

State v. Manning


No. A-3460-86T5F


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


234 N.J. Super. 147; 560 A.2d 693; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 254


April 11, 1989, Argued  
June 21, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]  Approved for Publication July 7, 1989. As Amended July 25, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Somerset County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his Superior Court, Somerset County (New Jersey) convictions for two counts of felony murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:11-3a(3), first-degree robbery, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:15-1a(1)(b), and second-degree criminal escape, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:29-5(a) and (d), arguing that the trial court unconstitutionally denied state-paid expert services for his defense. The state appealed from the order merging defendant's conviction for escape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of felony murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:11-3a(3), first-degree robbery, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:15-1a(1)(b), and second-degree criminal escape, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:29-5(a) and (d). The court affirmed his convictions, but remanded for resentencing. Case law obligated the trial court to conclude that, because defendant did not avail himself of state-appointed counsel, he could not receive state-funded expert services to assist in his defense. The court rejected this notion and held that under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2A:158A-14 defendant did not have to be represented by a public defender to receive the state's services because the right to such services hinged only on defendant's indigent status. However, the trial court's decision to deny state-funded services was still correct because the facts of the case did not warrant such services. The matter was remanded for resentencing based on the state's cross-appeal because the trial court erred in merging defendant's escape conviction.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed because it was proper for the trial court to have denied defendant's request for funds for expert services where the facts of the case did not warrant such services. However, the matter was remanded for resentencing because the trial court erred in merging defendant's escape conviction.

Gladstone v. Berk


Nos. A-1611-87T7, A-1612-87T7, A-1613-87T7 (Consolidated)


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


233 N.J. Super. 228; 558 A.2d 512; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 189


January 24, 1989, Argued  
May 17, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication May 31, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landowner's spouse and father appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County (New Jersey), which found in favor of plaintiff purchasers in their lawsuit seeking to compel defendant spouse to sell her dower interest in the land and to extinguish defendant father's interest in it.


OVERVIEW: Defendant landowner's spouse appealed a judgment, issued in plaintiff purchasers' lawsuit, compelling her to sell her dower interest in certain land that her spouse had contracted to sell. In addition, defendant landowner's father appealed the default judgment entered in favor of plaintiffs, pursuant to which his interest in the land was deemed extinguished. The court reversed insofar as defendant spouse was concerned and remanded insofar as defendant father was concerned. As to defendant spouse, the court held that she had an interest in the property, dower inchoate, which she could not be compelled to sell since she was not a party to the sales contract. As to defendant father, the default judgment rendered against him was set aside, and the case remanded for further proceedings, since it had been entered prior to the deadline by which he had to answer plaintiffs' complaint.


OUTCOME: The judgment compelling defendant spouse to sell her dower interest in certain land conveyed to plaintiff purchasers was reversed, because she was never a party to the sales contract. The default judgment with respect to defendant father was reversed and remanded, inasmuch as default was entered before he responded.

State v. Matthews

Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which convicted him under one indictment of possession of cocaine and possession of that substance with intent to distribute and under another indictment of possession of cocaine, and distribution of cocaine. Defendant was sentenced to 12 years imprisonment made consecutive to any violation of parole and 5 years imprisonment, consecutive to the prior sentence.


OVERVIEW: Following his guilty plea, defendant was convicted on two indictments of various drug offenses. The court concluded that if defendant desired to be sentenced under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-1c(2), and his application was granted, the state would be given an opportunity to make any appropriate application for extended terms treatment and defendant would be subject to any mandatory sentencing provisions required under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-1c(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence but remanded the cause for further proceedings on defendant's application for sentencing under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act, because the trial court mistakenly exercised his discretion by denying defendant's application on the grounds stated.

State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Dalton


No. A-4037-87T5


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


234 N.J. Super. 128; 560 A.2d 683; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 274


April 10, 1989, Submitted  
May 3, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication June 21, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, chiropractor and insured, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Bergen County (New Jersey), which required appellant chiropractor to submit a verification/certification of treatment as required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:33A-1 et seq. and N.J. Admin. Code tit. 11, § 16-1.1 et seq. in respondent insurer's action to compel compliance with tit. 11, § 16-1.1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurer filed an action against appellants, chiropractor and insured, to compel respondent chiropractor to submit a verification/certification of care pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:33A-1 et seq. and that he permit inspection of his records. The trial court granted respondent's request, and appellants sought review. Appellants argued that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:33A-6(b) was not applicable because appellant chiropractor was not requesting payment from respondent, and that because § 17:33A-6(b) was repealed it was a nullity. The court affirmed and held that because appellant chiropractor was appellant insured's husband, there was the likelihood of fraud, and thus, respondent was entitled to non-oppressive discovery as of right. Further, the court held that respondent chiropractor was required to comply with N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:6A-13 because his services were the subject of respondent insured's medical expenses claim. Finally, the court held that respondent insured was required to submit to a physical examination pursuant to § 39:6A-13(d) because the order adequately protected her legitimate religious interests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that the lower court's order compelling respondent chiropractor to submit a verification/certification of care was justified because respondent chiropractor was the subject of respondent insured's medical expenses claim. Further, respondent insured was required to submit to a physical examination because by its order, the lower court protected her religious interests.

Prudential Property and Cas. Ins. Co. v. Myers


No. A-965-88T5


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 455; 557 A.2d 686; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 150


March 21, 1989, Submitted  
April 24, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication May 11, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for declaratory relief, defendant insured and driver sought review of a summary judgment from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County (New Jersey), which granted a motion by plaintiff insurer that the territorial restriction of uninsured motorist coverage contained in its policy was enforceable. Defendants argued that the policy's territorial restriction was impermissible under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:28-1.1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant driver was injured when he was struck by an uninsured vehicle in British West Indies. Defendant driver was named as an additional insured in defendant insured's policy. Plaintiff insurer denied a claim to arbitrate damages for bodily injury because uninsured motorist (UM) coverage under the policy did not extend beyond the United States, its territories or possessions, or Canada. In a declaratory action to enforce the territorial restriction, plaintiff was granted summary judgment on the ground that the restriction was permitted under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:28-1.1. On appeal, defendants argued that § 17:28-1.1 should not have been interpreted to permit the restriction. The court disagreed, finding that the plain language of § 17:28-1.1 mandated UM coverage only within the United States and Canada. The court affirmed the summary judgment for plaintiff holding that the restriction was enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff insured that held that the territorial restriction of its uninsured motorist coverage contained in defendant insured's policy was enforceable. Plaintiff obtained declaratory relief after defendant driver was struck by an uninsured motorist in British West Indies and defendants filed a claim to arbitrate damages for bodily injury.

Council of City of Newark v. James


No. A-3278-87T7


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 449; 557 A.2d 683; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 157


February 16, 1989, Argued  
April 24, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication May 9, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city council challenged the judgment by the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), which concluded that plaintiff had standing to bring an action to determine which parcels of city-owned property should have been ordered for sale at public auction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city council challenged the decision by the trial court that determined that although they had standing to bring an action to determine which parcels of city-owned real property should have offered for sale at public auction and to determine the conditions to be attached to each sale, defendant mayor alone had the authority in question. The court affirmed the finding that plaintiff had standing but reversed the finding that defendant had the authority in question. Under the Local Lands and Building Law, N. J. Stat. Ann. § 40A:12-1 et seq., the function of choosing what city-owned real property to sell and attaching conditions was explicitly assigned to plaintiff and it was unnecessary to decide whether that function was administrative or legislative.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and found plaintiff city council had the authority to bring an action to determine which parcels of city-owned property should have been ordered for sale at public auction and to determine the conditions attached to each sale and plaintiff. Plaintiff had the function of authorizing sale of real property and the conditions related to the sale, as it was not an administrative function for defendant mayor.

Estate of Darrin v. Director of Div. of Taxation (Transfer Inheritance Tax Bureau)


No. A-2124-87T8


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 437; 557 A.2d 677; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 153


February 6, 1989, Argued  
April 21, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication May 8, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Tax Court of New Jersey, whose opinion is reported at 9 N.J. Tax 419 (Tax Ct. 1987).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, Director of the Division of Taxation, challenged the order by the Tax Court (New Jersey), which held that the use of gender-based mortality tables in evaluating the life estates was unconstitutional because it discriminated against women. Plaintiff estate of decedent cross-appealed the affirmance of a compromise tax that defendant assessed on the contingent remainder of the estate of the decedent's widow.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff estate of decedent filed a New Jersey inheritance tax return. A statement for direct taxes assessed was sent along with a suggested compromise tax on the contingent remainder in the life estate of decedent's widow. The Tax Court affirmed the compromise tax and found the use of gender-based mortality tables in evaluation of the remainder life estate unconstitutional because it discriminated against women. Cross motions for summary judgment were heard on the compromise tax assessed and on the finding that the statute was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the compromise tax, finding it was proper to base the compromise agreement on the tax rates at the time of decedent's death, and not on the rate in effect at the date of the distribution of the remainder interest. The court reversed the finding that gender-based mortality tables were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the compromise tax assessed on the contingent remainder interest in the life estate of widow of decedent plaintiff estate of decedent because it was assessed at the tax rates in effect at the time of decedent's death. The court reversed the finding that gender-based tables was unconstitutional because it was decided on less than a full record and such a decision would have had a significant impact in many areas of law.

State in Interest of A.J.


No. A-1366-88T1F


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Chancery Division, Family Part, Morris County.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed from an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Morris County, which waived jurisdiction over defendant and transferred the matter to another division of the court so that defendant would have been prosecuted as an adult for several controlled substances violations.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile and two minor females bought heroin and smoked marijuana cigarettes laced with the heroin. Defendant and one female abandoned the second female on train tracks where she had collapsed, and she was eventually struck and killed by a train. Defendant was charged in juvenile court with possession of heroin; possession of heroin with intent to distribute; distribution of heroin; and causing a drug-induced death. The court held that defendant failed to preserve his challenges to the constitutionality and applicability of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-9.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that waived jurisdiction over defendant juvenile and transferred the case against defendant to be prosecuted as an adult. The court held that the lower court's findings and conclusions in support of its determination to waive jurisdiction were amply supported by the proofs, and that defendant failed to preserve his constitutionality challenges to the statute permitting him to be tried as an adult.

Tp. of North Bergen v. City of Jersey City


Nos. A-2440-87T7, A-2465-87T7


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 219; 556 A.2d 1255; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 134


January 3, 1989, Argued  
April 13, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication April 27, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Hudson County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, township and town, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Hudson County (New Jersey), that found respondent city properly granted a tax abatement to respondent developer under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 55:14K-37(b), that found respondent developer's tax abatement was constitutional under N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 3, par. 1, and entered summary judgment in favor of respondents, city, developer, and tax board.


OVERVIEW: Respondent developer applied to respondent city for a tax abatement under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 55:14K-37(b). Respondent city held public meetings regarding the abatement and later adopted a resolution granting the abatement. Respondent developer entered into financing agreements with the New Jersey Housing Mortgage and Finance Agency and the agreements incorporated N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-3.3. Appellants, township and town, challenged the tax abatement and argued that it violated N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 3, par. 1. The court found that the steps taken by respondent city in passing the resolution for a tax abatement was in substance and effect an exercise of power by ordinance, and the tax abatement was constitutional.

Gregg v. Township Committee of Tp. of Hazlet


No. A-4176-87T5


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 34; 556 A.2d 348; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 118


February 7, 1989, Argued  
March 30, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication April 18, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mobile home park owners sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County (New Jersey), which denied plaintiff's request for counsel fees in their 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action against defendant township committee.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mobile home park owners sought and obtained a rent increase from defendant township committee. Tenants appealed to defendant, which considered the case during private executive session. Plaintiffs had neither the notice nor the opportunity to be heard at the session. Defendant later reduced the rent increase. Plaintiffs filed an action alleging that defendant had denied them due process violating 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, and that they were entitled to attorney fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The trial court found that plaintiffs' due process rights were violated, but denied attorney fees, finding that defendant acted in good faith and that the burden of the fees would be placed upon innocent taxpayers. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court. The reasons for denying the attorney fees advanced by the trial court were improper. Attorney fees should have been awarded as a matter of course. The good faith of defendant could not justify the denial of attorney's fees. The financial condition of the parties could not constitute special circumstances to defeat plaintiffs' entitlement to a fee award.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which found that plaintiff mobile home park owners were not entitled to an award of attorney fees after a finding that their due process rights were violated by defendant township committee. The reasons advanced by the trial court for denying the award were improper, and the court remanded the case for further consideration.

Holmdel Builders Ass'n v. Township of Holmdel


Nos. A-6063-86T8, A-22-87T8, A-5686-86T8, A-417-87T8, A-4958-86T7, A-3853-87T1 (Consolidated for purposes of appeal).


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


232 N.J. Super. 182; 556 A.2d 1236; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 120


January 18, 1989, Argued  
March 30, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication April 18, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from final judgments of the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth Morris and Middlesex Counties, State of New Jersey.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, townships and the Public Advocate, appealed from final judgments of the Superior Courts of New Jersey, Monmouth, Morris, and Middlesex Counties, which, in four separate actions, declared invalid appellant townships' ordinances exacting development fees as a condition to constructing residential and nonresidential units.


OVERVIEW: Appellant townships each adopted development fee ordinances in response to a judicial decision requiring municipalities to adopt land use regulations that created a realistic opportunity for the construction of low and moderate income housing. In four separate actions, appellee developers challenged the ordinances. The trial courts declared the ordinances facially invalid. On review, appellants, townships and the Public Advocate, contended that the development fee ordinances were sustainable because they were the "functional equivalent" to the Mount Laurel mandatory set-aside, and thus a valid land use regulation. The court affirmed in part, holding that three of the ordinances were unlawful taxes, which were imposed without legislative sanction and the development fees were not "functionally equivalent" to the mandatory set-aside technique. The court reversed respecting the validity of the fourth ordinance because it was not facially invalid due to the voluntary nature of the ordinance and a full factual inquiry was necessary to determine whether the voluntary development fee was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the trial court's judgment declaring invalid appellant townships' mandatory development fee ordinances because the ordinances were unlawful taxes which were imposed without legislative sanction. The court reversed and remanded in part for a plenary hearing respecting the validity of one ordinance because it was not facially invalid and a full factual inquiry was necessary to determine whether it was reasonable.

 

1105 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Crandall


No. A-5744 87T3


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


231 N.J. Super. 124; 555 A.2d 35; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 82


February 14, 1989, Submitted  
March 9, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication March 22, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Burlington County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction for first-degree aggravated assault, second-degree sexual assault, and endangering the welfare of a child from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Burlington County. The principal issues related to the requirements of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:84A-32.4, which allowed the victim to testify without face-to-face confrontation by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree sexual assault, second-degree sexual assault, and endangering the welfare of a child. The victim, who was 10 years old at the time of trial, testified through closed circuit television. Defendant challenged the conviction and the court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to conduct a supplementary N.J. R. Evid. 8 hearing to determine the possible distress the victim would have suffered if she had been forced to confront defendant at the time of trial. The court held that if expert testimony could be developed that, together with other evidence, resulted in clear and convincing proof that the victim was permitted to testify at trial pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:84A-32.4, an order confirming the judgment was required. If not, a new trial had to be ordered. The court held that although § 2A:84A-32.4 permitted a witness in a prosecution for certain crimes to be done through closed circuit television, a hearing had to be conducted first, and that where a defendant requested an expert evaluation of the witness in advance of the hearing, it had to be granted in the absence of specific reasons to justify denial of the request.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case for a hearing directed to emotional or mental distress the victim would have suffered if she had to confront defendant at the time of trial. If expert testimony was developed and resulted in clear and convincing proof that the closed circuit television testimony was proper, an order affirming the judgment of conviction for sexual assault and related charges was required. If not, a new trial had to be ordered.

Apple Chevrolet, Inc. v. Fair Lawn Borough


No. A-1494-87T7


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


231 N.J. Super. 91; 555 A.2d 17; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 67


October 26, 1988, Submitted  
February 27, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication March 20, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant car dealership sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Bergen County, which affirmed an adverse ruling to appellant's request for a variance from an ordinance that prohibited the loading and unloading of vehicles on the public street. Appellants had asserted that the ordinance was an invalid exercise of the police power.


OVERVIEW: Appellant car dealership challenged an ordinance prohibiting the loading and unloading of vehicles on public streets during deliveries to a car dealer, after its request for a variance claiming the ordinance was an invalid exercise of police power was denied by respondent zoning board. The trial court affirmed the board's decision, and the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court concluded that the challenge to the ordinance was not a matter to be considered by respondent zoning board under the sections of the Municipal Land Use Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-1 to -112, relating to land use variances, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-70(c), (d). The court determined that respondent borough passed the ordinance pursuant to its police power, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:48-1, not its zoning power, and that appellant could not challenge that ordinance's legality, as written or enforced, by a variance application under § 40:55D-70, but should have challenged the ordinance's legality by an action in lieu of prerogative or in defense of respondent borough's prosecution for violating the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that respondent zoning board did not have jurisdiction to entertain appellant car dealership's application for relief from the ordinance, because it was passed under respondent borough's police power. The court stated that it expressed no view on appellant's contention that the ordinance was invalid, and added that appellant could pursue that argument in an appropriate forum.

State v. One 1986 Subaru, N.J. Reg AXB-13X, Serial No. JFIAX45B3GB302047


No. A-3677-87T1


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


230 N.J. Super. 451; 553 A.2d 869; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 36


October 17, 1988, Argued  
February 9, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication February 22, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged dismissal of its complaint for failure to state a cause of action against respondent car owner, and respondent filed a cross-appeal of the denial by the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Essex County, of his motion to dismiss for failure to serve the summons and complaint as required by N.J. Ct. R. 4:4-1. Appellant claimed that the complaint stated a cause of action for forfeiture of respondent's car.


OVERVIEW: Respondent car owner was charged with the crime of causing death by auto. The state filed a complaint against respondent for forfeiture of the car under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:64-1 et seq. Respondent filed a cross-motion to dismiss the complaint because the state did not comply with N.J. Ct. R. 4:4-1. The trial court dismissed the complaint because it failed to state a cause of action as required by N.J. Ct. R. 4:6-2(e). The state challenged the decision, and respondent filed a cross-appeal. The court affirmed the dismissal. The court held that the car was driven recklessly, as defined in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:2-2b(3), and caused a death, as required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-5. Therefore, a forfeiture was proper as alleged in the complaint, and the complaint was improperly dismissed. However, the state's failure to issue the summons within 10 days of the filing of the complaint, as required by N.J. Ct. R. 4:4-1, had the clear capacity to prejudice respondent. Accordingly, the court concluded that the trial court erred in exercising its discretion by denying respondent's motion to dismiss the complaint, pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 4:37-2(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the state's complaint against respondent car owner for forfeiture of respondent's car. The court held that the complaint stated a cause of action but that the state's complaint should have been dismissed for failure of timely service of the summons and complaint. The trial judge abused his discretion in denying respondent's motion to dismiss.

Sendar v. State, Dept. of Human Services, Div. of Medical Assistance and Health Services


No. A-1870-87T8


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


230 N.J. Super. 537; 553 A.2d 1372; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 48


January 10, 1989, Argued  
February 6, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication February 27, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Morris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, shareholders and nursing home corporation, sought review of the order from the Superior Court of New Jersey in Morris County, which upheld a certificate of debt filed by defendants, division of the Department of Human Services and its director, as a judgment pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4D-17(h), but limited their effect as inchoate liens against plaintiffs' property.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, division of the Department of Human Resources (agency), and its director, filed certificates of debt pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4D-17(h) against plaintiff shareholders for Medicaid overpayments made to plaintiff nursing home corporation after its lease and physical assets were assigned to a new nursing home operator. The trial court dismissed the complaint and held that the certificates were inchoate liens placed against defendant shareholders' property that could not be executed until final agency adjudication of plaintiffs' responsibility. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the treatment of the filed certificates as interim liens satisfied U.S. Const. amend. XIV due process concerns because the temporary deprivation of plaintiffs' property was minor in relation to defendant's interest in the return of Medicaid overpayments and that alternatives to the filing of the certificates prior to hearings were not reasonable. However, the court held that filing the certificates against plaintiff shareholders was improper because defendant failed to show whether the individual plaintiffs received a benefit from the overpayments.


OUTCOME: The order upholding certificates of debt filed by defendants, division of the Department of Human Services and its director, was affirmed in part and reversed in part, because plaintiff shareholders suffered a temporary deprivation of their property interests in relation to defendants' interest in the return of Medicaid overpayments but that defendants failed to show that the plaintiffs individually benefited from the overpayments.

Murnane v. Murnane


No. A-3349-87T8


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


229 N.J. Super. 520; 552 A.2d 194; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 4


November 15, 1988, Submitted  
January 4, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]  As Amended January 3, 1989. Approved for Publication January 12, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Chancery Division, Family Part, Hunterdon County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother appealed the decision of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Hunterdon County, which entered a judgment of divorce of plaintiff and defendant father, insofar as it prevented plaintiff from moving with the child to Florida.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mother and defendant father were divorced and granted joint custody of their child with the primary residential custody remaining with plaintiff. Plaintiff appealed the judgment insofar as it prevented her from moving with the child to Florida. The trial court found that, while the move might have been beneficial to plaintiff, it would have proven detrimental to the child's relationship with defendant. The court reversed the trial court. The court held that defendant clearly had a significant connection with the state; therefore the jurisdictional provisions of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:34-31 were complied with. The court held that the trial court should have considered whether it would have been possible to honor defendant's visitation schedule while recognizing plaintiff's right to move. The court noted that defendant's reduction in child support could have helped finance transportation. The court also held that the trial court should have weighed the burden which defendant would have suffered if he was forced to relocate in order to remain close to the child against the burden plaintiff would have had to bear if she was forced to remain in New Jersey.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court preventing plaintiff mother from moving with her child to Florida and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that the trial court should have considered whether it would have been possible to honor defendant father's visitation schedule while recognizing plaintiff's right to move.

 

1110 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ogar


Nos. A-4292-87T5, A-4342-87T3


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


229 N.J. Super. 459; 551 A.2d 1037; 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 5


December 6, 1988, Argued  
January 3, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication January 12, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Morris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the decision of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Morris County, which dismissed charges against two defendants under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7, which prohibited distributing or possessing drugs within 1,000 feet of a school.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed counts for distributing or possessing drugs within a defined school zone in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7 against a defendant who was arrested for smuggling drugs into a jail that was located within 1,000 feet of a school and against a defendant who was pulled over by police in front of a school. The trial court held that § 2C:35-7's 1,000-foot criteria was unconstitutional, that it was not intended to apply to offenses committed in a jail, and that it was not intended to apply to situations involving moving violations that happened to occur near a school. On consolidated appeal, the court reinstated the charges and remanded. The court held that § 2C:35-7 was constitutional and that the legislature intended it to set forth a bright line test based strictly on distance from a school. The court found that inquiry into the circumstances of a drug offense was irrelevant under § 2C:35-7's strict liability approach. The court found that the trial court's decision finding § 2C:35-7's proscription against distribution or possession of drugs within 1,000 feet of a school bus was inappropriate because that section of the statute was not before the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and reinstated the charges of possessing drugs within 1,000 feet of a school because the statute was constitutional and its bright line test precluded inquiry into the circumstances of the drug possession. The court held that the trial court erred in finding the statute prohibiting possession of drugs within 1,000 feet of a school bus unconstitutional because that offense was not before the court.

State v. Velez


Nos. A-1258-87T4, A-1261-87T4 (CONSOLIDATED)


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


229 N.J. Super. 305; 551 A.2d 540; 1988 N.J. Super. LEXIS 451


November 28, 1988, Submitted  
December 16, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:   [***1] Approved for Publication December 28, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court, Law Division, Somerset County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Somerset County, which sentenced them to concurrent prison terms on each of several counts in indictments charging violations of controlled dangerous substance laws arguing that they had a right to require sentencing under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1986, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested and indicted on charges of conspiracy, possession, and distribution of cocaine following an undercover investigation. They entered guilty pleas and were sentenced under the Controlled Dangerous Substance Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-1et seq. The offenses were committed four months prior to the effective date of the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1986 (CDRA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-1 et seq., and the sentences were imposed four months after its effective date. Defendants appealed their sentences on the grounds that they had a right to election of sentencing under the CDRA. The court affirmed the sentences. The court held that defendants could request sentencing under the CDRA for offenses committed prior to its effective date but were not entitled to have their requests granted. The court held that the sentences were sustainable under either statute. Also, the court found that the trial court properly weighed the aggravating factors against the mitigating factor of their lack of prior criminal histories and any presumption of non-incarceration was rebutted. The sentences were not excessive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that sentenced defendants under Controlled Dangerous Substance Act that was in effect prior to the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1986. The court held that defendants had an option to request and consent to sentencing under the CDRA, but were not automatically entitled to have their request granted.

State in Interest of L.M.

State v. Davis


No. A-3502-86T4


Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division


229 N.J. Super. 66; 550 A.2d 1241; 1988 N.J. Super. LEXIS 420

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior court of New Jersey, Law Division, Salem County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Salem County (New Jersey), which convicted defendant of two counts of sexual assault and two counts of endangering the welfare of a child, and sentenced him to two concurrent, indeterminate terms not to exceed seven years each.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from convictions of two counts of sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2b, and two counts of endangering the welfare of a child, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:24-4. Defendant contended, among other things, that the trial judge erroneously denied his motion for a judgment of acquittal. The two problems that were raised were that one child could tell about the crime, but not about the perpetrator, while the other identified defendant as the perpetrator, but could not testify about the crime. Defendant claimed that each witness was allowed to impermissibly bolster the missing part of the other witness' case. The court affirmed, holding that this was a classic case of circumstantial evidence and the jury had the right to draw reasonable inference therefrom.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for sexual assault and endangering the welfare of a child, but modified the sentence to show concurrent indeterminate terms not to exceed five years for two counts of sexual assault.
